//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/%28Ursula_von_der_Leyen%29_2019.07.16._Ursula_von_der_Leyen_presents_her_vision_to_MEPs_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-%28Ursula_von_der_Leyen%29_2019.07.16._Ursula_von_der_Leyen_presents_her_vision_to_MEPs_2_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="987" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:(Ursula_von_der_Leyen)_2019.07.16._Ursula_von_der_Leyen_presents_her_vision_to_MEPs_2_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Incumbent <a href="/wiki/Ursula_von_der_Leyen" title="Ursula von der Leyen">President von der Leyen</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Von_der_Leyen_Commission" title="Von der Leyen Commission">Von der Leyen Commission</a></div>\n<p>In 2019, <a href="/wiki/Ursula_von_der_Leyen" title="Ursula von der Leyen">Ursula von der Leyen</a> was appointed as the next President of the European Commission. She submitted the guidelines of her policy for 2019\xe2\x80\x932024 to the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> on 16 July 2019, following her confirmation. She had not been considered a likely candidate (in general, the elected candidate is determined, according to the results of the <a href="/wiki/Elections_to_the_European_Parliament" title="Elections to the European Parliament">European election</a>, as winner of internal election into the dominant European party known as "spitzenkandidat"). Her appointment was consensual between <a href="/wiki/European_Council" title="European Council">European Council</a> Members (<a href="/wiki/Manfred_Weber" title="Manfred Weber">Manfred Weber</a> was not considered the most suitable candidate). On 9 September, the <a href="/wiki/European_Council" title="European Council">European Council</a> declared a list of candidate-commissioners which has to be officially approved by the parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Powers_and_functions">Powers and functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Powers and functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The commission was set up from the start to act as an independent supranational authority separate from governments; it has been described as "the only body paid to think European".<sup id="cite_ref-Day_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Day-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The members are proposed by their member state governments, one from each. However, they are bound to act independently&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 free from other influences such as those governments which appointed them. This is in contrast to the Council of the European Union, which represents governments, the European Parliament, which represents <a href="/wiki/EU_citizen" class="mw-redirect" title="EU citizen">citizens</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Economic_and_Social_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic and Social Committee">Economic and Social Committee</a>, which represents organised civil society, and the <a href="/wiki/European_Committee_of_the_Regions" title="European Committee of the Regions">Committee of the Regions</a>, which represents local and regional authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Institutions_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Institutions-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Through <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Consolidated_version_of_the_Treaty_on_European_Union/Title_III:_Provisions_on_the_Institutions#Article_17" class="extiw" title="s:Consolidated version of the Treaty on European Union/Title III: Provisions on the Institutions">Article 17 of the Treaty on European Union</a> the commission has several responsibilities: to develop medium-term strategies; to draft legislation and arbitrate in the legislative process; to represent the EU in trade negotiations; to make rules and regulations, for example in competition policy; to draw up the <a href="/wiki/Budget_of_the_European_Union" title="Budget of the European Union">budget of the European Union</a>; and to scrutinise the implementation of the treaties and legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The rules of procedure of the European Commission set out the commission\'s operation and organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-rules_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rules-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Executive_power">Executive power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Executive power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a> came into force, the executive power of the EU was held by the council: it conferred on the Commission such powers for it to exercise. However, the council was allowed to withdraw these powers, exercise them directly, or impose conditions on their use.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> This aspect has been changed by the Treaty of Lisbon, after which the Commission exercises its powers just by virtue of the treaties. Powers are more restricted than most national executives, in part due to the commission\'s lack of power over areas like <a href="/wiki/Common_Foreign_and_Security_Policy" title="Common Foreign and Security Policy">foreign policy</a>&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 that power is held by the European Council, which some analysts have described as another executive.<sup id="cite_ref-dragoman_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragoman-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Considering that under the Treaty of Lisbon, the European Council has become a formal institution with the power of appointing the commission, it could be said that the two bodies hold the executive power of the EU (the European Council also holds individual national executive powers). However, it is the Commission that currently holds executive powers over the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dragoman_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragoman-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The governmental powers of the Commission have been such that some, including former <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Prime_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgian Prime Minister">Belgian Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Guy_Verhofstadt" title="Guy Verhofstadt">Guy Verhofstadt</a>, have suggested changing its name to the "European Government", calling the present name of the Commission "ridiculous".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_initiative">Legislative initiative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Legislative initiative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Commission differs from the other institutions in that it alone has <a href="/wiki/Legislative_initiative" class="mw-redirect" title="Legislative initiative">legislative initiative</a> in the EU. Only the commission can make formal proposals for legislation: they cannot originate in the legislative branches. Under the Treaty of Lisbon, no legislative act is allowed in the field of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Foreign_and_Security_Policy" title="Common Foreign and Security Policy">Common Foreign and Security Policy</a>. In the other fields, the Council and Parliament are able to request legislation; in most cases the Commission initiates on the basis of these proposals. This monopoly is designed to ensure coordinated and coherent drafting of <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of the European Union">EU law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> This monopoly has been challenged by some who claim the Parliament should also have the right, with most national parliaments holding the right in some respects.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> However, the Council and Parliament may request the commission to draft legislation, though the Commission does have the power to refuse to do so<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> as it did in 2008 over transnational collective conventions.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Under the Lisbon Treaty, EU citizens are also able to request the commission to legislate in an area via <a href="/wiki/European_Citizens%27_Initiative" title="European Citizens&#39; Initiative">a petition carrying one million signatures</a>, but this is not binding.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The commission\'s powers in proposing law have usually centred on economic regulation. It has put forward a large number of regulations based on a "<a href="/wiki/Precautionary_principle" title="Precautionary principle">precautionary principle</a>". This means that pre-emptive regulation takes place if there is a credible hazard to the environment or human health: for example on tackling <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and restricting <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetically modified organisms">genetically modified organisms</a>. The European Commission has committed EU member states to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutrality" title="Carbon neutrality">carbon neutrality</a> by 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> This is opposed to weighting regulations for their effect on the economy. Thus, the Commission often proposes stricter legislation than other countries. Owing to the size of the European market, this has made EU legislation an important influence in the global market.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recently the commission has moved into creating <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_European_Union#Criminal_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of the European Union">European criminal law</a>. In 2006, a <a href="/wiki/2006_C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire_toxic_waste_spill" class="mw-redirect" title="2006 C\xc3\xb4te d&#39;Ivoire toxic waste spill">toxic waste spill off the coast of C\xc3\xb4te d\'Ivoire</a>, from a European ship, prompted the commission to look into legislation against toxic waste. Some EU states at that time did not even have a crime against shipping toxic waste; this led the Commissioners <a href="/wiki/Franco_Frattini" title="Franco Frattini">Franco Frattini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stavros_Dimas" title="Stavros Dimas">Stavros Dimas</a> to put forward the idea of "ecological crimes". Their right to propose criminal law was challenged in the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> but upheld. As of 2007, the only other criminal law proposals which have been brought forward are on the <a href="/wiki/Proposed_directive_on_criminal_measures_aimed_at_ensuring_the_enforcement_of_intellectual_property_rights" title="Proposed directive on criminal measures aimed at ensuring the enforcement of intellectual property rights">intellectual property rights directive</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-E!Sharp0507_Legal_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E!Sharp0507_Legal-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and on an amendment to the 2002 counter-terrorism framework decision, outlawing terrorism\xe2\x80\x91related incitement, recruitment (especially via the internet) and training.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enforcement">Enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Once legislation is passed by the Council and Parliament, it is the commission\'s responsibility to ensure it is implemented. It does this through the member states or through its <a href="/wiki/Agencies_of_the_European_Union" title="Agencies of the European Union">agencies</a>. In adopting the necessary technical measures, the commission is assisted by committees made up of representatives of member states and of the public and private <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbies</a><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> (a process known in jargon as "<a href="/wiki/Comitology" title="Comitology">comitology</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, the commission is responsible for the implementation of the <a href="/wiki/EU_budget" class="mw-redirect" title="EU budget">EU budget</a>, ensuring, along with the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Auditors" class="mw-redirect" title="Court of Auditors">Court of Auditors</a>, that EU funds are correctly spent.\n</p><p>In particular the commission has a duty to ensure the treaties and law are upheld, potentially by taking member states or other institutions to the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">Court of Justice</a> in a dispute. In this role it is known informally as the "guardian of the treaties".<sup id="cite_ref-oispex_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oispex-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Finally, the Commission provides some <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_European_Union" title="Foreign relations of the European Union">external representation</a> for the Union, alongside the member states and the <a href="/wiki/Common_Foreign_and_Security_Policy" title="Common Foreign and Security Policy">Common Foreign and Security Policy</a>, representing the Union in bodies such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>. It is also usual for the President to attend meetings of the <a href="/wiki/G8" class="mw-redirect" title="G8">G8</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-oispex_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oispex-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="College">College</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: College">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For members of the current Commission, see <a href="/wiki/Von_der_Leyen_Commission" title="Von der Leyen Commission">Von der Leyen Commission</a>.</div>\n<p>The commission is composed of a College of "<a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner" title="European Commissioner">Commissioners</a>" of 27 members, including the President and vice-presidents. Even though each member is nominated on the basis of the suggestions made by the national governments, one per state, they do not represent their state in the commission.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_examine_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_examine-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In practice, however, they do occasionally press for their national interest.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Once proposed, the President delegates <a href="/wiki/List_of_European_Commission_portfolios" title="List of European Commission portfolios">portfolios</a> among each of the members. The power of a Commissioner largely depends upon their portfolio, and can vary over time. For example, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Education,_Training_%26_Culture" class="mw-redirect" title="European Commissioner for Education, Training &amp; Culture">Education Commissioner</a> has been growing in importance, in line with the rise in the importance of education and culture in European policy-making.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Another example is the <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner_for_Competition" title="European Commissioner for Competition">Competition Commissioner</a>, who holds a highly visible position with global reach.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_examine_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_examine-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Before the commission can assume office, the College as a whole must be approved by the Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Institutions_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Institutions-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Commissioners are supported by their <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_(European_Commission)" title="Cabinet (European Commission)">personal cabinet</a> who give them political guidance, while the <a href="/wiki/European_Civil_Service" title="European Civil Service">Civil Service</a> (the DGs, see below) deal with technical preparation.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointment">Appointment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Appointment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Commission_Room_(Open_Day)_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/European_Commission_Room_%28Open_Day%29_1.jpg/170px-European_Commission_Room_%28Open_Day%29_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/European_Commission_Room_%28Open_Day%29_1.jpg/255px-European_Commission_Room_%28Open_Day%29_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/European_Commission_Room_%28Open_Day%29_1.jpg/340px-European_Commission_Room_%28Open_Day%29_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="642" data-file-height="856" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:European_Commission_Room_(Open_Day)_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Floor 13 of the Berlaymont, Commission\'s meeting room</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_European_Commission" title="President of the European Commission">President of the Commission</a> is first proposed by the <a href="/wiki/European_Council" title="European Council">European Council</a>, following a <a href="/wiki/Voting_in_the_Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Voting in the Council of the European Union">Qualified Majority Vote</a> (QMV), taking into account the latest <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Elections in the European Union">Parliamentary elections</a>; that candidate then faces a formal election in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a>. If the European Parliament fails to elect the candidate, the European Council shall propose another within one month.<sup id="cite_ref-Article_17:7_TEU_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Article_17:7_TEU-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sch\xc3\xbctze_2012_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sch\xc3\xbctze_2012-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the selection of the President, and the appointment of the <a href="/wiki/High_Representative" class="mw-redirect" title="High Representative">High Representative</a> by the European Council, each <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner" title="European Commissioner">Commissioner</a> is nominated by their member state (except for those states who provided the President and High Representative) in consultation with the Commission President, who is responsible for the allocation of portfolios. The President\'s proposed College of Commissioners is then subject to hearings at the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> which will question them and then vote on their suitability as a whole. If the European Parliament submits a negative opinion of a candidate, the President must either reshuffle them or request a new candidate from the member state to avoid the College\'s outright rejection by the European Parliament. Once the College is approved by parliament, it is formally appointed following a QMV vote by the <a href="/wiki/European_Council" title="European Council">European Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Article_17:7_TEU_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Article_17:7_TEU-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the College\'s appointment, the President appoints a number of <a href="/wiki/Vice-President_of_the_European_Commission" title="Vice-President of the European Commission">Vice-Presidents</a> (the High Representative is mandated to be one of them) from among the commissioners. For the most part, the position grants little extra power to Vice-Presidents, except the first Vice-President who stands in for the President when he or she is away.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_examine_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_examine-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dismissal">Dismissal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Dismissal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The European Parliament can dissolve the College of Commissioners as a whole following a <a href="/wiki/Vote_of_no-confidence" class="mw-redirect" title="Vote of no-confidence">vote of no-confidence</a> but only the President can request the resignation of an individual Commissioner. However, individual Commissioners, by request of the council or Commission, can be compelled to retire on account of a breach of obligation(s) and if so ruled by the European Court of Justice (Art. 245 and 247, Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_styles">Political styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Political styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Barroso_Commission" title="Barroso Commission">Barroso Commission</a> took office in late 2004 after being delayed by objections from the Parliament, which forced a reshuffle. In 2007 the Commission increased from 25 to 27 members with the accession of Romania and Bulgaria who each appointed their own Commissioners. With the increasing size of the commission, Barroso adopted a more presidential style of control over the college, which earned him some criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, under Barroso, the commission began to lose ground to the larger member states as countries such as France, the UK and Germany sought to sideline its role. This has increased with the creation of the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_European_Council" title="President of the European Council">President of the European Council</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> There has also been a greater degree of <a href="/wiki/European_Commissioner#Politicisation" title="European Commissioner">politicisation within the Commission</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration">Administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_Civil_Service" title="European Civil Service">European Civil Service</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Androulla_Vassiliou2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Androulla_Vassiliou2.jpg/220px-Androulla_Vassiliou2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Androulla_Vassiliou2.jpg/330px-Androulla_Vassiliou2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Androulla_Vassiliou2.jpg/440px-Androulla_Vassiliou2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="541" data-file-height="606" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Androulla_Vassiliou2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cypriot politician <a href="/wiki/Androulla_Vassiliou" title="Androulla Vassiliou">Androulla Vassiliou</a> was European Commissioner for Education, Culture, Multilingualism and Youth between 2010 and 2014.</div></div></div>\n<p>The commission is divided into departments known as <a href="/wiki/Directorate-General" title="Directorate-General">Directorates-General</a> (DGs) that can be likened to departments or <a href="/wiki/Ministry_(government_department)" title="Ministry (government department)">ministries</a>. Each covers a specific policy area such as agriculture or justice and citizens\' rights or internal services such as human resources and translation and is headed by a director-general who is responsible to a commissioner. A commissioner\'s portfolio can be supported by numerous DGs; they prepare proposals for them and if approved by a majority of commissioners proposals go forward to the Parliament and Council for consideration.<sup id="cite_ref-Europa_Institutions_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa_Institutions-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Civil_Service" title="European Civil Service">Commission\'s civil service</a> is headed by a <a href="/wiki/Secretary-General_of_the_European_Commission" title="Secretary-General of the European Commission">Secretary General</a>. The position is currently held by <a href="/wiki/Ilze_Juhansone" title="Ilze Juhansone">Ilze Juhansone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The rules of procedure of the European Commission set out the commission\'s operation and organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-rules_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rules-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There has been criticism from a number of people that the highly fragmented DG structure wastes a considerable amount of time in <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/turf_war" class="extiw" title="wikt:turf war">turf wars</a> as the different departments and Commissioners compete with each other. Furthermore, the DGs can exercise considerable control over a Commissioner with the Commissioner having little time to learn to assert control over their staff.<sup id="cite_ref-LEM_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LEM-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Press_Releases_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Press_Releases-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to figures published by the commission, 23,803 persons were employed by the commission as officials and temporary agents in September 2012. In addition to these, 9230 "external staff" (e.g. Contractual agents, detached national experts, young experts, <a href="/wiki/Traineeship_scheme_of_the_European_Commission" title="Traineeship scheme of the European Commission">trainees</a> etc.) were employed. The single largest DG is the <a href="/wiki/Directorate-General_for_Translation_(European_Commission)" class="mw-redirect" title="Directorate-General for Translation (European Commission)">Directorate-General for Translation</a>, with a 2309-strong staff, while the largest group by nationality is Belgian (18.7%), probably due to a majority (17,664) of staff being based in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Press">Press</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Press">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Berlaymont_Press_Room.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Berlaymont_Press_Room.jpg/220px-Berlaymont_Press_Room.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Berlaymont_Press_Room.jpg/330px-Berlaymont_Press_Room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Berlaymont_Press_Room.jpg/440px-Berlaymont_Press_Room.jpg 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="486" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Berlaymont_Press_Room.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Press Room in the Berlaymont</div></div></div>\n<p>Communication with the press is handled by the <a href="/wiki/Directorate-General_Communication_(European_Commission)" class="mw-redirect" title="Directorate-General Communication (European Commission)">Directorate-General Communication</a>. The Commission\'s chief spokesperson is Eric Mamer who holds the midday <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">press</a> briefings, commonly known as the "Midday Presser". It takes place every weekday in the Commission\'s press room at the Berlaymont where journalists may ask questions to the Commission officials on any topic and legitimately expect to get an "on the record" answer for live TV. Such a situation is unique in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As an integral part of the Directorate-General for Communication, the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://ec.europa.eu/info/contact/press-services/press-contacts/press-contacts-spokespersons-service_en">Spokesperson\'s Service</a>, in coordination with the Executive Communication Adviser in the President\xe2\x80\x99s Cabinet, supports the President and Commissioners so that they can communicate effectively. On political communication matters, the chief spokesperson reports directly to the President of the European Commission.\n</p><p>It has been noted by one researcher that the press releases issued by the commission are uniquely political. A release often goes through several stages of drafting which emphasises the role of the commission and is used "for justifying the EU and the commission" increasing their length and complexity. Where there are multiple departments involved a press release can also be a source of competition between areas of the Commission and Commissioners themselves. This also leads to an unusually high number of press releases, and is seen as a unique product of the EU\'s political set-up.<sup id="cite_ref-Press_Releases_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Press_Releases-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>There is a larger press corps in Brussels than Washington, D.C.; in 2020, media outlets in every Union member-state had a Brussels <a href="/wiki/Correspondent" title="Correspondent">correspondent</a>. Although there has been a worldwide cut in journalists, the considerable press releases and operations such as <a href="/wiki/Europe_by_Satellite" title="Europe by Satellite">Europe by Satellite</a> and <a href="/wiki/EuroparlTV" title="EuroparlTV">EuroparlTV</a> leads many news organisations to believe they can cover the EU from these source and <a href="/wiki/News_agency" title="News agency">news agencies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In the face of high-level criticism,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> the Commission shut down <a href="/wiki/Presseurop" title="Presseurop">Presseurop</a> on 20 December 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legitimacy_and_criticism">Legitimacy and criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Legitimacy and criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>As the commission is the executive branch, candidates are chosen individually by the 27 national governments. Within the EU, the legitimacy of the commission is mainly drawn from the vote of approval that is required from the European Parliament, along with its power to dismiss the body. Eurosceptics have therefore raised the concern of the relatively low turnout (often less than 50%) in elections for the European Parliament <a href="/wiki/1999_European_Parliament_election" title="1999 European Parliament election">since 1999</a>. While that figure may be higher than that of some national elections, including the off-year elections of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, the fact that there are no direct elections for the position of commission president calls the position\'s legitimacy into question in the eyes of some Eurosceptics.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The fact that the commission can directly decide (albeit with oversight from specially formed <a href="/wiki/Comitology" title="Comitology">\'comitology committees\'</a>) on the shape and character of implementing legislation further raises concerns about democratic legitimacy.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even though democratic structures and methods are changing there is not such a mirror in creating a European <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lisbon" title="Treaty of Lisbon">Treaty of Lisbon</a> may go some way to resolving the perceived deficit in creating greater democratic controls on the commission, including enshrining the procedure of linking elections to the selection of the Commission president. Historically, the commission had indeed been seen as a technocratic expert body which, akin with institutions such as independent <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central banks</a>, deals with technical areas of policy and therefore ought to be removed from party politics.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> From this viewpoint, electoral pressures would undermine the commission\'s role as an independent regulator.<sup id="cite_ref-Majone_2002_375_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majone_2002_375-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Defenders of the Commission point out that legislation must be approved by the Council in all areas (the ministers of member states) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> in most areas before it can be adopted, thus the amount of legislation which is adopted in any one country without the approval of its government is limited.<sup id="cite_ref-Majone_2002_375_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Majone_2002_375-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2009 the European ombudsman published <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> of citizens\' complaints against EU institutions, with most of them filed against the commission (66%) and concerning lack of transparency (36%).<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 the commission was sued for blocking access to documents on EU biofuel policy.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> This happened after media accused the Commission of blocking scientific evidence against biofuel subsidies.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Lack of transparency, unclear lobbyist relations, conflicts of interests and excessive spending of the commission was highlighted in a number of reports by internal and independent auditing organisations.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> It has also been criticised on IT-related issues, particularly with regard to <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiatives">Initiatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Initiatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-terrorism">Anti-terrorism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Anti-terrorism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The European Commission has an Action Plan to enhance preparedness against <a href="/wiki/CBRN_defense" title="CBRN defense">chemical, biological, radiological and nuclear</a> (CBRN) security risks as part of its anti-terrorism package released in October 2017. In recent times Europe has seen an increased threat level of CBRN attacks. As such, the European Commission\'s preparedness plan is important, said Steven Neville Chatfield, a director for the Centre for Emergency Preparedness and Response in the United Kingdom\'s <a href="/wiki/Health_Protection_Agency" title="Health Protection Agency">Health Protection Agency</a>. For the first time, the European Commission proposed that medical preparedness for CBRN attack threats is a high priority. "The European Commission\xe2\x80\x99s (EC) Action Plan to enhance preparedness against CBRN security risks is part of its anti-terrorism package released in October 2017, a strategy aimed at better protecting the more than 511 million citizens across the 27 member states of the European Union (EU).\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COVID-19_response">COVID-19 response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: COVID-19 response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The European Commission organized a video conference of world leaders on 4 May 2020 to raise funds for coronavirus vaccine development. US$8 billion was raised. The United States declined to join this video conference or to contribute funds.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Location">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Belgique_-_Bruxelles_-_Schuman_-_Berlaymont_-_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Belgique_-_Bruxelles_-_Schuman_-_Berlaymont_-_01.jpg/220px-Belgique_-_Bruxelles_-_Schuman_-_Berlaymont_-_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Belgique_-_Bruxelles_-_Schuman_-_Berlaymont_-_01.jpg/330px-Belgique_-_Bruxelles_-_Schuman_-_Berlaymont_-_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Belgique_-_Bruxelles_-_Schuman_-_Berlaymont_-_01.jpg/440px-Belgique_-_Bruxelles_-_Schuman_-_Berlaymont_-_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5180" data-file-height="2894" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Belgique_-_Bruxelles_-_Schuman_-_Berlaymont_-_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Headquarters of the European Commission in Brussels (Berlaymont building)</div></div></div>\n<p>The commission is primarily based in Brussels, with the President\'s office and the commission\'s meeting room on the 13th floor of the <a href="/wiki/Berlaymont" class="mw-redirect" title="Berlaymont">Berlaymont</a> building. The commission also operates out of <a href="/wiki/Brussels_and_the_European_Union" title="Brussels and the European Union">numerous other buildings in Brussels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg_City" title="Luxembourg City">Luxembourg City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-European_Commission_locations_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-European_Commission_locations-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> When the Parliament is meeting in <a href="/wiki/Strasbourg" title="Strasbourg">Strasbourg</a>, the Commissioners also meet there in the <a href="/wiki/Seat_of_the_European_Parliament_in_Strasbourg#Secondary_buildings" title="Seat of the European Parliament in Strasbourg">Winston Churchill building</a> to attend the Parliament\'s debates.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the European Commission has facilities that support it in Alicante, Spain; Munich, Germany; and Varese, Italy.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=European_Commission&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r936637989">.mw-parser-output .portal{border:solid #aaa 1px;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portal.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portal>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output
